This is because high-pressure <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> is the most marketable product and <a href="/wiki/Pressure_swing_adsorption" title="Pressure swing adsorption">pressure swing adsorption</a> (PSA) purification systems work better at higher pressures. The product mixture is known as "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synthesis_gas" title="Synthesis gas">synthesis gas</a>" because it is often used directly for the production of <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a> and related compounds. <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">Hydrocarbons</a> other than methane can be used to produce synthesis gas with varying product ratios. One of the many complications to this highly optimized technology is the formation of coke or carbon:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> → C + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>Consequently, steam reforming typically employs an excess of <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>. Additional hydrogen can be recovered from the steam by use of carbon monoxide through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_gas_shift_reaction" title="Water gas shift reaction">water gas shift reaction</a>, especially with an <a href="/wiki/Iron_oxide" title="Iron oxide">iron oxide</a> catalyst. This reaction is also a common industrial source of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxtoby_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-Oxtoby-91">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>CO + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>Other important methods for <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> production include partial oxidation of hydrocarbons:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> → 2 CO + 4 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>and the coal reaction, which can serve as a prelude to the shift reaction above:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxtoby_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-Oxtoby-91">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>C + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> → CO + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>Hydrogen is sometimes produced and consumed in the same industrial process, without being separated. In the <a href="/wiki/Haber_process" title="Haber process">Haber process</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Ammonia_production" title="Ammonia production">production of ammonia</a>, hydrogen is generated from natural gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[91]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">Electrolysis</a> of <a href="/wiki/Brine" title="Brine">brine</a> to yield <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> also produces hydrogen as a co-product.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metal-acid">Metal-acid</span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Laboratory" title="Laboratory">laboratory</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> is usually prepared by the reaction of dilute <a href="/wiki/Oxidizing_acid" title="Oxidizing acid">non-oxidizing acids</a> on some reactive metals such as <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> with <a href="/wiki/Kipp%27s_apparatus" title="Kipp's apparatus">Kipp's apparatus</a>.
</p>
<dl><dd>Zn + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">Zn<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">Aluminium</a> can also produce <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> upon treatment with bases:
</p>
<dl><dd>2 Al + 6 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">OH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> → 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">Al(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + 3 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>An alloy of aluminium and <a href="/wiki/Gallium" title="Gallium">gallium</a> in pellet form added to water can be used to generate hydrogen. The process also produces <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alumina" title="Alumina">alumina</a>, but the expensive gallium, which prevents the formation of an oxide skin on the pellets, can be re-used. This has important potential implications for a hydrogen economy, as hydrogen can be produced on-site and does not need to be transported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thermochemical">Thermochemical</span></h3>
<p>There are more than 200 thermochemical cycles which can be used for <a href="/wiki/Water_splitting" title="Water splitting">water splitting</a>, around a dozen of these cycles such as the <a href="/wiki/Iron_oxide_cycle" title="Iron oxide cycle">iron oxide cycle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cerium(IV)_oxide%E2%80%93cerium(III)_oxide_cycle" title="Cerium(IV) oxide–cerium(III) oxide cycle">cerium(IV) oxide–cerium(III) oxide cycle</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zinc_zinc-oxide_cycle" title="Zinc zinc-oxide cycle">zinc zinc-oxide cycle</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulfur-iodine_cycle" title="Sulfur-iodine cycle">sulfur-iodine cycle</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copper-chlorine_cycle" title="Copper-chlorine cycle">copper-chlorine cycle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_sulfur_cycle" title="Hybrid sulfur cycle">hybrid sulfur cycle</a> are under research and in testing phase to produce hydrogen and oxygen from water and heat without using electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[94]</a></sup> A number of laboratories (including in France, Germany, Greece, Japan, and the USA) are developing thermochemical methods to produce hydrogen from solar energy and water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anaerobic_corrosion">Anaerobic corrosion</span></h3>
<p>Under anaerobic conditions, <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steel_alloy" title="Steel alloy">steel alloys</a> are slowly oxidized by the protons of water concomitantly reduced in molecular hydrogen (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>). The <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_corrosion" title="Anaerobic corrosion">anaerobic corrosion</a> of iron leads first to the formation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferrous_hydroxide" title="Ferrous hydroxide">ferrous hydroxide</a> (green rust) and can be described by the following reaction:
</p>
<dl><dd>Fe + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>O → <span class="chemf nowrap">Fe(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>In its turn, under anaerobic conditions, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferrous_hydroxide" title="Ferrous hydroxide">ferrous hydroxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Fe(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) can be oxidized by the protons of water to form <a href="/wiki/Magnetite" title="Magnetite">magnetite</a> and molecular hydrogen.
This process is described by the <a href="/wiki/Schikorr_reaction" title="Schikorr reaction">Schikorr reaction</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd>3 <span class="chemf nowrap">Fe(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">Fe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>O + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></dd>
<dd><i>ferrous hydroxide → magnetite + water + hydrogen</i></dd></dl>
<p>The well crystallized magnetite (<span class="chemf nowrap">Fe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>) is thermodynamically more stable than the ferrous hydroxide (<span class="chemf nowrap">Fe(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>).
</p><p>This process occurs during the anaerobic corrosion of <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> in <a href="/wiki/Anoxic_waters" title="Anoxic waters">oxygen-free</a> <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a> and in reducing <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soils</a> below the <a href="/wiki/Water_table" title="Water table">water table</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geological_occurrence:_the_serpentinization_reaction">Geological occurrence: the serpentinization reaction</span></h3>
<p>In the absence of atmospheric oxygen (<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>), in deep geological conditions prevailing far away from Earth atmosphere, hydrogen (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) is produced during the process of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serpentinization#Hydrogen_production_by_anaerobic_oxidation_of_fayalite_ferrous_ions" title="Serpentinization">serpentinization</a> by the anaerobic oxidation by water protons (H<sup>+</sup>) of the ferrous (Fe<sup>2+</sup>) silicate present in the crystal lattice of <a href="/wiki/Fayalite" title="Fayalite">fayalite</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Fe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>, the <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a> iron-endmember). The corresponding reaction leading to the formation of <a href="/wiki/Magnetite" title="Magnetite">magnetite</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Fe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz</a> (Si<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) and hydrogen (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) is the following:
</p>
<dl><dd>3<span class="chemf nowrap">Fe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>O → 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">Fe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + 3 Si<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + 3 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></dd>
<dd><i>fayalite + water → magnetite + quartz + hydrogen</i></dd></dl>
<p>This reaction closely resembles the <a href="/wiki/Schikorr_reaction" title="Schikorr reaction">Schikorr reaction</a> observed in anaerobic oxidation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferrous_hydroxide" title="Ferrous hydroxide">ferrous hydroxide</a> in contact with water.
the
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_in_transformers">Formation in transformers</span></h3>
<p>From all the fault gases formed in power <a href="/wiki/Transformer" title="Transformer">transformers</a>, hydrogen is the most common and is generated under most fault conditions; thus, formation of hydrogen is an early indication of serious problems in the transformer's life cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consumption_in_processes">Consumption in processes</span></h3>
<p>Large quantities of <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> are needed in the petroleum and chemical industries. The largest application of <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> is for the processing ("upgrading") of fossil fuels, and in the production of <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>. The key consumers of <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> in the petrochemical plant include <a href="/wiki/Hydrodealkylation" title="Hydrodealkylation">hydrodealkylation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrodesulfurization" title="Hydrodesulfurization">hydrodesulfurization</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrocracking" title="Hydrocracking">hydrocracking</a>. <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> has several other important uses. <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> is used as a hydrogenating agent, particularly in increasing the level of saturation of unsaturated fats and <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil" title="Vegetable oil">oils</a> (found in items such as margarine), and in the production of <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>. It is similarly the source of hydrogen in the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a>. <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> is also used as a <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing agent</a> of metallic <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ores</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hydrogen is highly soluble in many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rare_earth_element" title="Rare earth element">rare earth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metals</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Takeshita_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Takeshita-100">[98]</a></sup> and is soluble in both nanocrystalline and <a href="/wiki/Amorphous_metal" title="Amorphous metal">amorphous metals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kirchheim1_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kirchheim1-101">[99]</a></sup> Hydrogen <a href="/wiki/Solubility" title="Solubility">solubility</a> in metals is influenced by local distortions or impurities in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crystal_lattice" title="Crystal lattice">crystal lattice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kirchheim2_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kirchheim2-102">[100]</a></sup> These properties may be useful when hydrogen is purified by passage through hot <a href="/wiki/Palladium" title="Palladium">palladium</a> disks, but the gas's high solubility is a metallurgical problem, contributing to the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_embrittlement" title="Hydrogen embrittlement">embrittlement</a> of many metals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rogers_1999_1057–1064_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rogers_1999_1057–1064-12">[11]</a></sup> complicating the design of pipelines and storage tanks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christensen_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Christensen-13">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Apart from its use as a reactant, <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> has wide applications in physics and engineering. It is used as a <a href="/wiki/Shielding_gas" title="Shielding gas">shielding gas</a> in <a href="/wiki/Welding" title="Welding">welding</a> methods such as <a href="/wiki/Atomic_hydrogen_welding" title="Atomic hydrogen welding">atomic hydrogen welding</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[102]</a></sup> H<sub>2</sub> is used as the rotor coolant in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_generator" title="Electrical generator">electrical generators</a> at <a href="/wiki/Power_station" title="Power station">power stations</a>, because it has the highest <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a> of any gas. Liquid H<sub>2</sub> is used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryogenic" title="Cryogenic">cryogenic</a> research, including <a href="/wiki/Superconductivity" title="Superconductivity">superconductivity</a> studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[103]</a></sup> Because <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> is lighter than air, having a little more than ​<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>14</sub></span> of the density of air, it was once widely used as a <a href="/wiki/Lifting_gas" title="Lifting gas">lifting gas</a> in balloons and <a href="/wiki/Airship" title="Airship">airships</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Almqvist03_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Almqvist03-106">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>In more recent applications, hydrogen is used pure or mixed with nitrogen (sometimes called <a href="/wiki/Forming_gas" title="Forming gas">forming gas</a>) as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tracer_gas" title="Tracer gas">tracer gas</a> for minute leak detection. Applications can be found in the automotive, chemical, power generation, aerospace, and telecommunications industries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[105]</a></sup> Hydrogen is an authorized food additive (E 949) that allows food package leak testing among other anti-oxidizing properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hydrogen's rarer isotopes also each have specific applications. <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">Deuterium</a> (hydrogen-2) is used in <a href="/wiki/CANDU_reactor" title="CANDU reactor">nuclear fission applications</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_moderator" title="Neutron moderator">moderator</a> to slow <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_5-11"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-5">[5]</a></sup> Deuterium compounds have applications in chemistry and biology in studies of reaction <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_isotope_effect" title="Kinetic isotope effect">isotope effects</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[107]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">Tritium</a> (hydrogen-3), produced in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a>, is used in the production of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bomb" title="Hydrogen bomb">hydrogen bombs</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[108]</a></sup> as an isotopic label in the biosciences,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-holte_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-holte-59">[57]</a></sup> and as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beta_radiation" title="Beta radiation">radiation</a> source in luminous paints.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Triple_point" title="Triple point">triple point</a> temperature of equilibrium hydrogen is a defining fixed point on the <a href="/wiki/International_Temperature_Scale_of_1990" title="International Temperature Scale of 1990">ITS-90</a> temperature scale at 13.8033 <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">Kelvin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coolant">Coolant</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen-cooled_turbo_generator" title="Hydrogen-cooled turbo generator">Hydrogen-cooled turbo generator</a></div>
<p>Hydrogen is commonly used in power stations as a coolant in generators due to a number of favorable properties that are a direct result of its light diatomic molecules. These include low <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a>, low <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a>, and the highest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_heat_capacity" title="Specific heat capacity">specific heat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a> of all gases.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_carrier">Energy carrier</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_economy" title="Hydrogen economy">Hydrogen economy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_infrastructure" title="Hydrogen infrastructure">Hydrogen infrastructure</a></div>
<p>Hydrogen is not an energy resource,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sustain_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-sustain-113">[111]</a></sup> except in the hypothetical context of commercial <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> power plants using <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a>, a technology presently far from development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[112]</a></sup> The Sun's energy comes from <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">nuclear fusion</a> of hydrogen, but this process is difficult to achieve controllably on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[113]</a></sup> Elemental hydrogen from solar, biological, or electrical sources requires more energy to make than is obtained by burning it, so in these cases hydrogen functions as an energy carrier, like a battery. Hydrogen may be obtained from fossil sources (such as methane), but these sources are unsustainable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sustain_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-sustain-113">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> per unit <i>volume</i> of both <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">liquid hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Compressed_hydrogen" title="Compressed hydrogen">compressed hydrogen</a> gas at any practicable pressure is significantly less than that of traditional fuel sources, although the energy density per unit fuel <i>mass</i> is higher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sustain_113-2"><a href="#cite_note-sustain-113">[111]</a></sup> Nevertheless, elemental hydrogen has been widely discussed in the context of energy, as a possible future <i>carrier</i> of energy on an economy-wide scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[114]</a></sup> For example, <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CO2_sequestration" title="CO2 sequestration">sequestration</a> followed by <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Carbon capture and storage">carbon capture and storage</a> could be conducted at the point of <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> production from fossil fuels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GATech_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-GATech-117">[115]</a></sup> Hydrogen used in transportation would burn relatively cleanly, with some <a href="/wiki/NOx" title="NOx">NO<sub>x</sub></a> emissions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[116]</a></sup> but without carbon emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GATech_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-GATech-117">[115]</a></sup> However, the infrastructure costs associated with full conversion to a hydrogen economy would be substantial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[117]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fuel_cell" title="Fuel cell">Fuel cells</a> can convert hydrogen and oxygen directly to electricity more efficiently than internal combustion engines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garbak11_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-garbak11-120">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Semiconductor_industry">Semiconductor industry</span></h3>
<p>Hydrogen is employed to saturate broken ("dangling") bonds of <a href="/wiki/Amorphous_silicon" title="Amorphous silicon">amorphous silicon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amorphous_carbon" title="Amorphous carbon">amorphous carbon</a> that helps stabilizing material properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[119]</a></sup> It is also a potential <a href="/wiki/Electron_donor" title="Electron donor">electron donor</a> in various oxide materials, including <a href="/wiki/Zinc_oxide" title="Zinc oxide">ZnO</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[121]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tin_dioxide" title="Tin dioxide">SnO<sub>2</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Cadmium_oxide" title="Cadmium oxide">CdO</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_oxide" title="Magnesium oxide">MgO</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[122]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zirconium_dioxide" title="Zirconium dioxide">ZrO<sub>2</sub></a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hafnium(IV)_oxide" title="Hafnium(IV) oxide">HfO<sub>2</sub></a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lanthanum(III)_oxide" title="Lanthanum(III) oxide">La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Yttrium(III)_oxide" title="Yttrium(III) oxide">Y<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Titanium_dioxide" title="Titanium dioxide">TiO<sub>2</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Strontium_titanate" title="Strontium titanate">SrTiO<sub>3</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Lanthanum_aluminate" title="Lanthanum aluminate">LaAlO<sub>3</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">SiO<sub>2</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_oxide" title="Aluminium oxide">Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Zircon" title="Zircon">ZrSiO<sub>4</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Hafnon" title="Hafnon">HfSiO<sub>4</sub></a>, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Strontium_zirconate&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Strontium zirconate (page does not exist)">SrZrO<sub>3</sub></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_reactions">Biological reactions</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Biohydrogen" title="Biohydrogen">Biohydrogen</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_hydrogen_production_(Algae)" title="Biological hydrogen production (Algae)">Biological hydrogen production (Algae)</a></div>
<p>H<sub>2</sub> is a product of some types of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">anaerobic metabolism</a> and is produced by several <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a>, usually via reactions <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalyzed</a> by <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>- or <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>-containing <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> called <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenase" title="Hydrogenase">hydrogenases</a>. These enzymes catalyze the reversible <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">redox</a> reaction between H<sub>2</sub> and its component two protons and two electrons. Creation of hydrogen gas occurs in the transfer of reducing equivalents produced during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyruvate" title="Pyruvate">pyruvate</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">fermentation</a> to water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[124]</a></sup> The natural cycle of hydrogen production and consumption by organisms is called the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_cycle" title="Hydrogen cycle">hydrogen cycle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rhee6_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rhee6-127">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Water_splitting" title="Water splitting">Water splitting</a>, in which water is decomposed into its component protons, electrons, and oxygen, occurs in the <a href="/wiki/Light-dependent_reactions" title="Light-dependent reactions">light reactions</a> in all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photosynthetic" title="Photosynthetic">photosynthetic</a> organisms. Some such organisms, including the alga <i><a href="/wiki/Chlamydomonas_reinhardtii" title="Chlamydomonas reinhardtii">Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a>, have evolved a second step in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dark_reaction" title="Dark reaction">dark reactions</a> in which protons and electrons are reduced to form H<sub>2</sub> gas by specialized hydrogenases in the <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">chloroplast</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[126]</a></sup> Efforts have been undertaken to genetically modify cyanobacterial hydrogenases to efficiently synthesize H<sub>2</sub> gas even in the presence of oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[127]</a></sup> Efforts have also been undertaken with genetically modified <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_hydrogen_production_(Algae)" title="Biological hydrogen production (Algae)">alga in a bioreactor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety_and_precautions">Safety and precautions</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_safety" title="Hydrogen safety">Hydrogen safety</a></div>

<td><a href="/wiki/NFPA_704" title="NFPA 704">NFPA 704</a>
</td>
<td><div style="width:100%; background:transparent;"><div id="container" style="margin:0 auto; width:82px; font-family:sans-serif"><div class="nounderlines" id="on_image_elements" style="background:; float:left; font-size:20px; text-align:center; vertical-align:middle; position:relative; height:80px; width:80px; padding:1px;">
<div id="diamond_image_and_mw_ImageMap" style="position:absolute; height:80px; width:80px;"><div class="noresize"><map name="ImageMap_1_875294492"><area alt="Flammability code 4: Will rapidly or completely vaporize at normal atmospheric pressure and temperature, or is readily dispersed in air and will burn readily. Flash point below 23 °C (73 °F). E.g., propane" coords="40,0,60,20,40,40,20,20" href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Red" shape="poly" title="Flammability code 4: Will rapidly or completely vaporize at normal atmospheric pressure and temperature, or is readily dispersed in air and will burn readily. Flash point below 23 °C (73 °F). E.g., propane"/><area alt="Health code 0: Exposure under fire conditions would offer no hazard beyond that of ordinary combustible material. E.g., sodium chloride" coords="20,20,40,40,20,60,0,40" href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Blue" shape="poly" title="Health code 0: Exposure under fire conditions would offer no hazard beyond that of ordinary combustible material. E.g., sodium chloride"/><area alt="Reactivity code 0: Normally stable, even under fire exposure conditions, and is not reactive with water. E.g., liquid nitrogen" coords="60,20,80,40,60,60,40,40" href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Yellow" shape="poly" title="Reactivity code 0: Normally stable, even under fire exposure conditions, and is not reactive with water. E.g., liquid nitrogen"/><area alt="Special hazards (white): no code" coords="40,40,60,60,40,80,20,60" href="/wiki/NFPA_704#White" shape="poly" title="Special hazards (white): no code"/></map><img alt="NFPA 704 four-colored diamond" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" height="80" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/NFPA_704.svg/80px-NFPA_704.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/NFPA_704.svg/120px-NFPA_704.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/NFPA_704.svg/160px-NFPA_704.svg.png 2x" usemap="#ImageMap_1_875294492" width="80"/></div></div><div style="width:12px; text-align:center; position:absolute; top:12px; left:35px;">
<a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Red" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Flammability code 4: Will rapidly or completely vaporize at normal atmospheric pressure and temperature, or is readily dispersed in air and will burn readily. Flash point below 23 °C (73 °F). E.g., propane">4</span></a></div><div style="width:13px; text-align:center; position:absolute; top:31px; left:15px;">
<a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Blue" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Health code 0: Exposure under fire conditions would offer no hazard beyond that of ordinary combustible material. E.g., sodium chloride">0</span></a></div><div style="width:13px; text-align:center; position:absolute; top:31px; left:54px;">
<a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Yellow" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Reactivity code 0: Normally stable, even under fire exposure conditions, and is not reactive with water. E.g., liquid nitrogen">0</span></a></div></div></div></div>
</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>Hydrogen poses a number of hazards to human safety, from potential <a href="/wiki/Detonation" title="Detonation">detonations</a> and fires when mixed with air to being an <a href="/wiki/Asphyxia" title="Asphyxia">asphyxiant</a> in its pure, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>-free form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAH2_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASAH2-132">[130]</a></sup> In addition, liquid hydrogen is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryogen" title="Cryogen">cryogen</a> and presents dangers (such as <a href="/wiki/Frostbite" title="Frostbite">frostbite</a>) associated with very cold liquids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[131]</a></sup> Hydrogen dissolves in many metals, and, in addition to leaking out, may have adverse effects on them, such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_embrittlement" title="Hydrogen embrittlement">hydrogen embrittlement</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[132]</a></sup> leading to cracks and explosions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[133]</a></sup> Hydrogen gas leaking into external air may spontaneously ignite. Moreover, hydrogen fire, while being extremely hot, is almost invisible, and thus can lead to accidental burns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cunn88_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cunn88-136">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>Even interpreting the hydrogen data (including safety data) is confounded by a number of phenomena. Many physical and chemical properties of hydrogen depend on the <a href="/wiki/Spin_isomers_of_hydrogen" title="Spin isomers of hydrogen">parahydrogen/orthohydrogen</a> ratio (it often takes days or weeks at a given temperature to reach the equilibrium ratio, for which the data is usually given). Hydrogen detonation parameters, such as critical detonation pressure and temperature, strongly depend on the container geometry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAH2_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASAH2-132">[130]</a></sup>
</p>

See <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">286 kJ/mol: energy per mole of the combustible material (molecular hydrogen)</span>
</li>
</ol></div>
